Reversed stomach occurs when food intake is excessive but ends with the morning meal being vomited in the evening, or the evening meal being vomited in the morning, or vomiting occurring after one or two meals, or after accumulating for a day and night, leading to unbearable bloating in the abdomen and subsequent vomiting of the original substances in an undigested, sour, and foul state. This means that it has entered the stomach and is now being expelled, hence it is called reversed stomach. This condition can occur in men and women of all ages.[49p]
    The condition known as "Guan Ge" is described as being unable to eat even a grain of rice; when thirsty, one prefers to drink tea or water, but soon feels the need to expel it. Repeated attempts to drink lead to further expulsion. When taking medicine, hot medicine is immediately rejected, and cold medicine takes a longer time to be expelled. There is also constipation, referred to as Guan Ge. "Guan" means that substances cannot exit the lower part, while "Ge" indicates that substances cannot enter the upper part. This condition is often seen in women.

    Regarding the obstruction caused by food in the throat, Danxi claims it arises from the seven emotions and six desires, leading to a transformation of heat rising and a lack of descent. This causes a buildup of phlegm and fluid, providing temporary relief during moments of discomfort, only to resurge soon after. When previous medicines are taken again, they accumulate heat, and blood and fluid become depleted, leading to a dry and withered state in the stomach area. When dryness occurs near the throat, water can pass through, but food is difficult to consume, leading to a condition known as "Yè" or obstruction. If this dryness is located nearer to the stomach, food can enter but is hard to retain, leading to a long delay before it exits the body, referred to as "Ge" or reversed stomach. Bowel movements are infrequent, resembling the consistency of sheep feces.[50p]
    One must avoid the six excesses externally and regulate the seven emotions internally. By nurturing oneself with appropriate food and drink to nourish the blood and generate fluids, the body will be free from the flames of heat, the kidneys will gradually produce water, and when the qi is clear and the blood is harmonious, the spleen will function well, allowing for the digestion and transformation of food.

    Danxi's discussions are quite clever, but his explanations about choking and reversed stomach conditions lack clarity. I particularly appreciate his idea of the fiery transformation rising up and the kidneys gradually producing water, as it keenly identifies the source of illness. However, I regret that his understanding remains somewhat superficial; he emphasizes nourishing the blood but does not directly explore the original essence within the kidneys. Therefore, his treatment methods involve formulas rich in animal milk, along with substances like bamboo sap and leek juice, which resolve phlegm and stasis but address only the symptoms rather than the root cause.

    Does one not understand that the "Neijing" originally does not have much discourse but simply states that the three yang conditions signify a blockage? The three yang refer to the large intestine, small intestine, and bladder, and blockage signifies heat accumulation. The large intestine governs fluids, while the small intestine governs liquids. If there is heat accumulation in the large intestine, fluids are depleted; if there is heat accumulation in the small intestine, liquids dry up. The bladder is the governing official of the region, storing fluids. If there is heat accumulation in the bladder, the fluids are exhausted.[51p]
    However, how does the "Three Yangs" lead to heat accumulation? All of it is due to kidney disease. The kidneys govern the five bodily fluids and regulate both urination and defecation; they are considered one organ with the bladder. When kidney water is depleted and yang fire becomes excessive, it boils the fluids, resulting in heat accumulation of the "Three Yangs." This leads to blockage both anteriorly and posteriorly, causing difficulties in excretion; hence, it must counteract upward, impacting the pathways of clarity, and overflows into the throat, causing difficulty in swallowing food.

    Why can water be ingested while food is hard to swallow? Food enters the yin aspect and nourishes the yang, which elevates the fire in the stomach, making it hard to intake; water, being more yin in nature, resonates well and thus can be ingested. When one spits white foam, it is due to the water they consumed boiling and rising. When feces resemble sheep droppings, it indicates that little food has been consumed, and the residue has been fully absorbed, resulting in dry and narrow intestines.

    These symptoms are often found in men over the age of fifty who have developed such conditions externally. Moreover, such individuals usually do not curb their desires. If inquiring about the cause, they might shy away from discussing their ailments and instead cite recent emotional distress, experiencing much stagnation as a result. It is my belief that this stagnation certainly exists, and they may indulge in activities to alleviate their worries, which is often the case, perhaps 80 to 90 percent of the time, and it may not require further elaboration.[52p]
    However, the old man's innocence has long disappeared, leaving only the solitary yang, focusing primarily on nourishing yin. Wang Taifu said: If food is ingested and then vomited, it indicates a lack of water; if food is retained for a long time and then expelled, it indicates a lack of fire. A lack of water hinders the mastery of water, and a lack of fire diminishes the source of fire. Zhu Shizhong stated: To treat an upper ailment, one must use large doses of Liuwei Dihuang pill decocted for drinking; long-term use can recover roughly one-tenth or one-twentieth. It is also necessary to abstain from indulging desires, avoiding certain rooms, and maintaining a bland diet. If one claims to warm the stomach, the stomach is inherently not cold; if one claims to tonify the stomach, the stomach is not deficient; if one claims to alleviate stagnation, aromatic and dry items should not assist fire. The "Jufang Feihua" already has clear explanations, stating that Liu from Hejian uses purgative methods which can damage the stomach with excessive cold, leading to further depletion of fluids. There's no remedy like nourishing yin, which extinguishes the flame. The common people do not understand, and thus I elaborate in detail.[53p]
    Regarding vomiting, the "Essentials from the Golden Chamber" states: If the pulse at the Fuyang point is superficial and weak, being superficial indicates deficiency, and being weak harms the spleen. If the spleen is harmed, digestion does not occur, leading to morning nausea and evening vomiting, or evening meals resulting in morning vomiting, with undigested food, a condition known as vomiting.

    I came across a medical case in historical texts stating that a patient with vomiting had undigested food coming up until the next morning. The doctor administered warming gastric medicine, but it was ineffective, and the pulse was very faint and weak. A national expert examined the case and noted that the medicines used were not addressing the patient's condition effectively, leaving the doctor perplexed.

    Reading Dong Yuan’s writings, it is stated that vomiting has three causes: accumulation of qi, cold, and cold in the lower region. Upper jiao vomiting is due to qi, middle jiao vomiting is due to accumulation, and lower jiao is due to cold. In this case, the pulse is deep and slow, with morning nausea and evening vomiting, and small bowel movements but constipation. This indicates lower jiao vomiting. The treatment should focus on relieving the blockage and warming the cold. Therefore, we specifically treat the lower jiao, dispelling the cold, and gradually harmonizing with middle jiao remedies to promote recovery.

    From this, we see that lower jiao vomiting is due to weakened fire at the gate of life, like a pot without fuel, unable to simmer the fluids and food in the stomach, leading to bloating and inevitable vomiting.[54p]
    The so-called prolonged consumption leading to depletion is indeed a lack of fire. One must utilize the original source of beneficial fire, first supplementing the Mingmen fire with Bawei Dihuang Wan to support the Earth of the Spleen, and then gradually using Fuzhu to administer the Lichong Decoction to regulate the interior, ensuring everything is well taken care of. Not understanding this, one merely uses hawthorn or Shenqu to flatten the stomach and digest food, which only hastens demise.

    Regarding the condition of "Guan Ge," it arises suddenly and manifests as a violent illness. Symptoms include constipation, thirst for water, occasional vomiting, alternating between drinking and vomiting, dry lips, slightly red eyes, and either a flushed or unflushed face. In severe cases, there may be chest pain or not, while the patient loses all desire to eat from the onset of illness, unable to swallow even a drop of water, and vomiting half a cup after drinking one. After several days, the pulse becomes submerged. This cold enters from the Shaoyin Kidney channel, with Yin flourishing below and pushing Yang upward, known as the symptom of "Ge Yang," termed "Guan Ge." Those with Guan Ge cannot use their full vitality and may die.[55p]
    One should use Zhang Jing's Bai Tong Decoction, following the method of using cold due to heat as stated in the Inner Canon. The text says: If one adjusts the contra-reversals of cold and heat, then the cold will surely act on the hot substances. After swallowing, once the cold nature is removed, the heat nature will begin to manifest, leading to the resolution of the illness, and vomiting will cease, thereby bringing significant benefits without any adverse effects. This combines the human urine and the bile of pigs, which are salty, bitter, and cold in nature, into the Bai Tong Decoction. The qi must correspond to one another to dispel the cold that obstructs. If the pulse gradually emerges after taking the medicine, life is likely; if the pulse suddenly emerges, death is imminent. Tao Jie'an's "Kai Che Chui" contains a remarkable recipe for "Hui Yang Fan Ben Decoction." After recovery, one should regularly take the Ba Wei Pill to prevent recurrence.

    Moreover, there is a type of liver fire condition where vomiting occurs but nothing can be retained; the vomit consists of sour water, bitter water, or blue-green water, while bowel movements remain normal. It can also cause heart pain. This is a case of stagnant fire and wood. The stagnation of wood must be resolved and the fire released. One should use a concentrated decoction of Fructus Ziziphi Jujubae and Radix Gentianae, sipping it slowly. Then take Xiao Yao San for recovery. After recovery, one should use the Liu Wei Wan for regulation.[56p]
    The Laws of Medicine
    By Yu Chang (Qing Dynasty)

    On the Theory of Closing the Cadres

    The proof of closing the cadres is deeply discussed in the "Ling," "Su," and "Nan Jing," as well as in Zhongjing's pulse methodology, yet there are no established methods for it. Later generations, lacking concrete methods to rely upon, have been left with abstract discussions of subtle meanings without thorough research, merely preserving its name for a long time. Occasionally, some, without the guidance of a master, formulate prescriptions based on their own understanding and pass them down in texts, but they remain as fleeting gold dust in their eyes, unsuitable for practical use. What can one do about this? I humbly express this with a respectful discussion of the underlying principles. The "Su Wen" states that the manifestation of a major illness in the lesser yang is represented by one sign, in the greater yang by two, in the yangming by three, and anything beyond that is classified as closing the yang; in the cun-kou (pulse), a major illness in the jueyin is shown by one sign, in the shaoyin by two, in the taiyin by three, and anything beyond that is classified as closing the yin.[57p]
    When the pulse at the Renying and the Cun position is strong, if it exceeds four times, it indicates a blockage. A weakened pulse at the blockage cannot reach the essence of the universe and will lead to death. This is related to the three Yang organs and the three Yin organs, which are diagnosed at the positions next to the throat's Renying, and at the positions of the hands' Cun and Taiyuan. The needling methods are applied according to the flow of the Renying and Cun meridians. The "Spiritual Pivot" states that acupuncture should address the excess Yang by draining it and supplementing the corresponding Yin, employing two drainages and one supplementation. Similarly, for the Cun, excess Yin is drained while supplementing the corresponding Yang, also using two drainages and one supplementation, all of which should cease when the Qi is harmonized. Regarding herbal treatment, it is important to assess the pulses at the wrists' Cun, Guan, and Chi positions to differentiate the Yin and Yang of the organs. Therefore, the "Spiritual Pivot" further states that when pathology is in the organs, the Yang pulse is out of harmony; when the Yang pulse is out of harmony, Qi stagnates, and when Qi stagnates, Yang Qi becomes excessive. If Yang Qi becomes too abundant, the Yin pulse will be out of harmony; when the Yin pulse is out of harmony, blood stagnates, and when blood stagnates, Yin Qi becomes excessive.[58p]
    When the yin qi is too abundant, the yang qi cannot thrive; thus it is called a "barrier." When the yang qi is too abundant, the yin qi cannot thrive; thus it is called a "block." When both yin and yang are abundant, they cannot nurture each other; hence it is referred to as a "barrier-block." A barrier-block cannot allow one to die at a predetermined time. This is the balance used in medicine, determining treatment according to the pulse's measurements of excessive yin and yang. The people of Yue regard this as significant, expressing it as a pulse where yin dominates over yang. They interpret excess yang as being like overflow and entry as coverage, depicting the phenomenon of yin and yang being skewed and unable to revert accurately. Later, Zhongjing established three major principles: he stated that a floating and large pulse at the cun position indicates emptiness, while the size indicates solidity; at the chi position, it is a barrier, and at the cun, it is a block. If there is a barrier, one cannot urinate easily, and with a block, there may be vomiting. By examining both hands at the cun position, determining the balance between excessive yin and yang, and observing whether they are floating or large, one can ascertain if there is deficiency in yang or excess in yin, or vice versa, to apply treatment.[59p]
    In the "Lingshu," it is stated that when Yang is excessively strong, Yin cannot thrive; when Yin is excessively strong, Yang cannot thrive. Furthermore, the methods of the Yue people concerning Yin and Yang, coupled with the distinctions of vacuity and substance, lead to a clear understanding of reality. Without this, the conditions will be firmly categorized as Yin or Yang. But from whence can one discern the subtle nuances? This is one principle: when the heart pulse is ample and long, it indicates the essence of the heart’s pulse. If the upper pulse is slight and the head small, sweating occurs; if the lower pulse is large, then obstruction results, and urination cannot take place. If there is no sweating in the head, treatment is possible; if sweating occurs, death follows. This clearly illustrates the source of the obstruction, which stems from the fire of the five emotions being trapped within the heart's membrane. The upper pulse appearing slight with a small head indicates Yang deficiency, while the lower pulse appearing large indicates Yang excess. If there is no sweating in the head, treatment is feasible; if there is sweating, the heart’s essence is lost, leading to self-immolation. In cases of the two Yang conditions affecting the heart and spleen, there can be no hidden curves; men may have diminished essence, and women may be without their monthly cycle, resulting in transmitted conditions that vanish without remedy.[60p]
    The condition of "guan ge" is characterized by exhaustion of vital energy, deterioration of the body's form, separation from and obstruction of essence, and the feelings of sorrow, fear, and anger. The five internal organs become empty, energy and blood are detached, while the fire of the yang essence moves independently. This affects the heart and spirit, all contained within a tiny space, where the chances of life and death are exceedingly close—how can one not carefully discern it? These are the two methods. It refers to the presence or absence of the "fu yang" pulse; when it is suppressed, it leads to regurgitation, and food and drink cannot be digested. When it is weak, no food can even enter, which is called "guan ge." By diagnosing the "fu yang" pulse at the foot, one can determine whether it is suppressed or weak, which reveals the state of the stomach's qi. If suppressed, it indicates that food and drink enter but do not digest; if weak, it shows that no food can enter, revealing the state of the stomach's qi even further. When the flow of nourishment and defenses is slow, and the intake of food and drink is scarce, while the central system is not functioning, how could one wait for further explanation? These are the three methods. The refined techniques of Zhang Zhongjing, through the "golden needle," provide insights through these three methods; fundamentally, they are concerned with the state of deficiency.[61p]
    Given this text spans ancient and modern times, it searches through various opinions, and among renowned scholars and sages, none discuss this evidence, only mentioning that Qizi described the conditions of yin and yang being opposed, transmitting what he tested across nine regions. This is akin to navigating the seas for thousands of miles, finding a companion with whom to share a harmonious voice, bringing unmatchable joy, yet one cannot consider it a shortcoming. However, one does not wish future generations to settle comfortably into these views, nor can one bear to remain silent. It is said that when yin and yang shift places, the condition is termed as "blockage." If yang energy is consistently present in the chest, it leads to heat as the primary illness; if yin energy is consistently present below the waist, it leads to cold as the primary illness. If there is cold in the chest, one should treat it with heat; if there is heat in the lower dantian, one should treat it with cold. If there is a combination of cold and heat in the chest, treatment should be based on the primary and secondary principles. The treatment for the primary condition should be gentle, while treatment for the secondary condition should be urgent. This stems from the "Treatise on Cold Damage," where cold is present in the chest and heat in the lower dantian, asserting the viewpoint that is indeed not necessarily linked to blockage as a fundamental condition. The method of treating primary and secondary conditions referenced from the "Inner Canon" also lacks a solid basis.[62p]
    As for the texts of "Ling," "Su," "Nan Jing," and "Jin Gui," they are utterly incomprehensible. The established prescriptions have been muddled into the poor practices of later generations, and upon examination, they are quite displeasing to the ear.

    In the formulas, there are minor flaws, such as the mixed use of Ban Xia, Wu Ling, Zhi Ke, Hou Po, Bing Lang, and Mu Xiang. The major flaws involve the mixed use of Pian Nao, She Xiang, Fu Zi, Zao Jiao, Qian Niu, Da Huang, and Shi Bo Nitrate.

    Can it be said that yin and yang do not intersect, each creating its own bias, while yin is claimed to be above and yang below? Can the vital signs of near-death be seized and dictated by dominant techniques influencing yin and yang?

    By using Fu Yang as a diagnostic tool, Zhang Jing aimed for harmony in the guarding and nutritive Qi, seeking neither bias toward yin nor toward yang, advocating for a balanced approach without confrontation, allowing the stomach's Qi to naturally assert itself. From one to nine, and then to eight, seven, four, and six, one may begin to achieve equilibrium. Can this be accomplished in one stroke?

    Thus, regardless of how to initiate treatment or the means by which it is communicated, all should seek from within, grasp the pivot and maneuver, gradually penetrating from top to bottom, waiting for the Fu Yang pulse to neither collapse nor weaken. When nutritive Qi moves forward, attention is added to it; when guarding Qi moves forward, attention is added to it, leading efforts in accordance with their momentum, so as not to conflict with the medicines.[63p]
    When the nutritive and protective energies are harmoniously connected, one should seek to balance them. The protective energy, when in harmony, should also be sought for balance. In pursuit of life, one should rejoice in accomplishments; how can one not desire this when overwhelmed by restlessness? The practice of seeking life within death requires individuals who are experienced in navigating such circumstances.

    To address loss, one must first extinguish the flames of the five emotions. To treat reflux, one must gradually address it from the interior and allow it to surface; to manage urinary obstruction, one must likewise address it from within and allow it to manifest below. Treatment requires an understanding of both the channels and the points, moving gradually from the interior to influence both upper and lower concerns.

    The saying goes: "If the moxa of three years is not retained, one cannot escape death; if retained, one can avoid death." Why then should people not practice retention? Some might be ill, and I do not propose a singular approach, for those who lack understanding of treatable methods may remain perpetually ineffective. In dire situations, one may reluctantly resort to a formula involving huanglian, but that should not become the standard approach for the average person.[64p]
    Discussion on the Prescription of Huanglian Decoction for Ingress and Egress

    Huanglian Decoction is a formula from Zhongjing for treating typhoid fever. When there is heat in the chest,邪气 in the stomach, and pain in the abdomen with a desire to vomit, Huanglian Decoction is the primary remedy. The邪气 in the stomach obstructs the mechanism of ascent and descent of yin and yang, preventing them from interacting with the middle earth. Thus, yin cannot rise and becomes trapped below, causing cold pain in the lower abdomen; yang cannot descend and remains solely in the upper body, leading to heat in the chest and a desire to vomit.

    This decoction indeed facilitates the proper ascent and descent of yin and yang. However, for those with dampness, a tongue that resembles a fetus, heat in the dantian, and cold in the chest, can this formula still be used? Later generations have forced interpretations that fail to capture the essence of the formula, yet how can they modify the prescription? Typhoid fever is typically divided into three treatments: exterior, interior, and central. When both exterior and interior邪气 are strong, one should harmonize from the center, which is why there is the harmonizing method of Xiao Chaihu Decoction, combining Ren Shen, Gan Cao, Ban Xia, Sheng Jiang, and Da Zao to support the stomach, while adding one ingredient of Chaihu to facilitate the exterior and one ingredient of Huangqin to penetrate the interior.[65p]
    There is still a fear that the essence may be insufficiently exposed, which can be detrimental to harmony. Thus, after removing the dregs, it is repeatedly boiled without distinction, and when consumed, it enters the stomach. The ascending stomach qi carries Chai Hu to the exterior, while the descending stomach qi brings Huang Qin inward. When they harmonize, the evils from both interior and exterior are dispelled; if any remain, they should be treated accordingly, without any conflict.

    As for the evils within the lower dantian and the chest, they are situated above and below and are not considered exterior or interior. Therefore, one should transform Chai Hu Decoction into Huang Lian Decoction to harmonize these areas. Replace Chai Hu with Gui Zhi, Huang Qin with Huang Lian, and fresh ginger with dried ginger. When consumed, it is also important to monitor the upward and downward movements of the stomach qi, allowing for treatment regardless of whether the upper part is hot and the lower part is cold, or vice versa.

    For exterior and interior evils, Chai Hu and Huang Qin should be used; for upper and lower evils, Gui Zhi and Huang Lian are applied. To dispel exterior evils, fresh ginger's pungency is used; for upper and lower evils, the spiciness of dried ginger is employed. The methods of Zhang Zhongjing are indeed profound. If one wishes to engage in the methods of promoting or retreating the upper and lower, what technique should one use? In previous discussions, these were sought through the middle, grasping the pivot and moving it, gradually penetrating from above and below, waiting for the ying qi to be fully communicated and the wei qi to be fully obtained for advancement or retreat. However, this aspect is difficult to articulate. If there is obstruction, there might be regurgitation; thus, using this method is appropriate. After all, the sun (Tai Yang) governs opening; if the sun does not open, there will be obstruction in the chest, preventing food from being ingested, which would either be refused entry or expelled again. Therefore, using Gui Zhi as the medicinal agent of the Tai Yang channel harmonizes ying and wei qi and facilitates the Yang pathway, thus enabling the opening.[66p]
    As for the five emotions and the yang fire rising, the use of cinnamon twig (gui zhi) is no longer appropriate; if used, it will lead to fire assisting fire, causing sweating from the head and the escape of yang, thus preventing urination. The method of retreating relies on the stomach qi penetrating into the yin segment, hence cinnamon twig should not be utilized. However, if the stomach's gate is already closed, the lesser yin governs closure, and the qi of the lesser yin does not rise, the stomach’s gate cannot open. Chang Yi stated that the pulse of the lesser yin is deep and stagnant, while that of the foot yang is prone and slack—both are causes for concern. The Nei Jing often reiterates this: when the kidney qi is solely deep, it is said to be unbalanced. The true qi in the kidney is akin to a balance scale; if there is both weight and balance, the gate opens and closes at intervals; if there is weight but no balance, the gate is closed and cannot open. How then can the small urine be released? Thus, the kidney qi pill embodies all matters of retreating. When the kidney qi connects with the stomach, the gate opens; when it connects with the heart, the yang fire proceeds to descend, making it sometimes unavoidable to use. Advance and retreat must follow this order; is this something an average person can discern?[67p]
    In the treatment of obstructive diseases, if one does not know how to differentiate between suppression and promotion, but only hopes to stop vomiting and improve urination, hastily addressing the superficial symptoms, then exhausting all efforts without benefit is the fault of the physician. In treating obstructive diseases, if one does not consider the three pulses—Renying, Fuyang, and Taichong—and focuses solely on the inch pulse, this is already a gross oversight. If one also lacks clarity in distinguishing between the yin and yang aspects of the inch pulse, this is the fault of the physician.

    In treating obstructive diseases, if one does not discern the differences between yang deficiency and fullness, or yin deficiency and fullness, and administers treatment blindly, like a blind person on a road without knowing east from west, this is the fault of the physician. In treating obstructive diseases, if one does not adhere to noble principles and instead employs forceful techniques to flaunt their abilities, pushing the patient toward death, this is the fault of the physician.[68p]
    Complete Collection of Ancient and Modern Books, Medical Section, Volume 313

    Yue Ge and Counter-Effects

    Jingyue's Complete Works [Ming Dynasty, Zhang Jiebin]

    Discussion on Guan Ge

    The condition of "guan ge" is illustrated in the "Inner Canon," which discusses the pulse patterns to reveal the dangers of the separation of Yin and Yang. This is evident from discussions found in various sections such as the "Six Seasonal Organs," "Beginning and End," "Prohibitions on Medicines," and discussions regarding pulse measurements and meridians. Since these insights are so significant, we can understand their weight.

    Originating from the writings of the Qin dynasty's Yue people, there are three difficulties mentioned: "Upper fish" indicates overflow, signifying the external blockage in "guan"; "Inner measurement" indicates overturning, signifying the internal blockage in "ge." Discussing "guan ge" in these terms has strayed from their original meaning in the canon.

    Moreover, Zhongjing states: at the pulse point of the "chi" (尺) is "guan," while at the pulse point of the "cun" (寸) is "ge." If "guan" is affected, urination is impaired; if "ge," then there is vomiting and regurgitation. Thus, from the time of Shuhe and Dongyuan onwards, this understanding has been tradition, and eventually, the notion of "guan ge" has been lost entirely.

    Later, Danxi mentioned: this condition often leads to death. Cold is located above and heat below, with the pulse in both "cun" points being four times stronger than normal; the treatment should involve emesis to elevate the horizontal stagnation of Qi without needing to expel phlegm.[69p]
    I foolishly argue that if both the cun and the two-inch pulse are four times strong, how can we then say that it indicates cold in excess? Moreover, if the pulse is large like this, it is floating and has no root, its emptiness is evident; how can it be expelled? This is erroneous and indeed the worst error! The Inner Classic states that the renying pulse can be four times strong, and the cunkou pulse can also be four times; these are not to be taken literally about their size, but rather indicate an expulsion of food stagnation; to say one cannot urinate indicates urinary retention. Each of these two conditions has its own criteria, and how does it relate to the diagnosis of guangu? This is true for you students—why should it be different in other cases? How can we then expect those who treat such conditions to be free from error?

    The guangu diagnosis in the Inner Classic is originally examined through the renying pulse for the yang of the six fu organs, and the cunkou pulse examines the yin of the five zang organs. When the renying pulse is exceedingly strong, exceeding four times, this indicates the solitary yang of the yangming channel; the water does not assist the fire, hence it is termed gu阳. Gu阳 refers to the yin being constrained by the yang. When the cunkou pulse is exceedingly strong, exceeding four times, this indicates that the ta阴 channel has no main yin, and the qi does not return to the essence, hence it is termed gu阴. Gu阴 refers to the yang intervening with the yin. If both the renying and cunkou pulses are strong to the level of four times or more, both large and numerous, this indicates that the yang qi does not accumulate, thus there is no yang within the yin, and the yin qi does not rise, hence there is no yin within the yang; yin and yang are separated, hence it is named guangu.

    All those who observe this condition are generally affected by excessive drinking and indulgence, and emotional desires harm the essence; thus, the yang cannot maintain its residence, leading to a floating pulse that is exposed and excessively strong. This signifies the true yin being depleted and the yuan sea having no root, which genuinely reflects the image of a dragon soaring high with regrets, indicating the utmost danger in prognosis.[70p]
    The "Inner Canon" uses the pulse at the inch and the renying to diagnose, but in later generations, practitioners only use the inch and neglect the renying, indicating that the method is incomplete. The inch pulse is the major representation of the pulse, and when the pulse is discerned there, it will also appear in the other; hence, by observing the inch, the pulse of the renying can also be generally seen. Therefore, whenever the inch pulse is extremely large, even four times its expected size, and both large and numerous, it indicates a pulse of guan ge, which should not be mistaken for a fire condition. I have diagnosed several individuals with this pulse, and upon observation, it felt like a string or leather, abnormally large—hence I say four times. Observing its symptoms, the pulse movement corresponds with body movement, particularly noticeable in the voids beneath the breasts and in the motions around the navel; all appear active and resonant, responding with the pulse. In examining its form and energy, there is slight wheezing above, and upon movement, the wheezing intensifies. The limbs lack strength, and there are frequent states of anxiety during wakefulness and sleep, leading one to deem it a deficiency without cough or blood loss symptoms, or if judged to be phlegm-fire, it lacks signs of a true pathogenic heat. This is what differentiates guan ge. However, only wealthy individuals and those with a robust physique tend to exhibit these symptoms.[71p]
    To seek the cause, it is nothing but an excess of pleasure in the youthful and middle years, with wine and lust as the culprits. Although this differs from the symptoms of overstrain, it is indeed another name for it. Therefore, those of mature age should be cautious!

    The "Classic on Pulse Diagnosis" states: When yin qi is too abundant, yang qi cannot flourish, hence it is called 'Guan' (Barrier); when yang qi is too abundant, yin qi cannot flourish, hence it is called 'Ge' (Obstruction). When both yin and yang are robust and cannot support each other, it is also called 'Guan Ge'. The state of 'Guan Ge' means one cannot reach a timely death. This addresses pulse symptoms comprehensively.

    If one speaks in terms of pulse, it resembles the prior four-fold conditions. If one speaks in terms of symptoms, there are cases where both yin and yang are vigorous, with a yang condition extreme in its yang aspect and a yin condition extreme in its yin aspect.

    In general, when yang is exceeding in yang, one should proceed to purge, yet if the yin aspect reveals itself, it cannot be purged; when yin is extreme in yin, one should proceed to replenish, yet if the yang aspect reveals itself, it cannot be replenished. In such instances, yang exists solely in the yang without any yin, and yin exists solely in the yin without any yang. They are isolated, unable to interact, and thus cannot be supplemented nor purged. This is also a symptom of 'Guan Ge' and leads to death.

    This is significantly different from the symptoms of true cold with false heat or true heat with false cold—students should distinguish between the similar yet different manifestations.

    Discussion on treatment.[72p]
    The pulse of the "Guan Ge" must be extremely taut. The tautness represents a deficiency in the middle, while the floating and large indicate a deficiency in yin. This pulse indicates severe depletion of kidney water, where yang is present but yin is lacking. Those treating this condition should primarily focus on strongly replenishing the true yin. Additionally, one must observe the cold and heat within the deficiency, as well as the balance of yin and yang, and treat accordingly for optimal results.

    In "Guan Ge" syndrome, if there is a combination with yang organs, there will be excess heat, so treatments like Yinxian Decoction, Left Kidney Decoction, and Left Kidney Pill should be administered. If there is a combination with yin organs, there will be excess cold, thus treatments like Great Harmony Decoction, Right Kidney Decoction, and Right Kidney Pill should be used. If there is neither heat nor cold, and the organ's energy is fundamentally balanced, then Five Blessings Decoction, Three Yin Decoction, and Great Yuan Supplementing Decoction should be prescribed.

    In cases of "Guan Ge" syndrome, if the underlying damage is severe, medications and food must not be neglected. For those with essence deficiency, support should be provided for their essence; for those with qi deficiency, support should be given for their qi. If there are complex conditions that are difficult to detail, one should select appropriate prescriptions from ancient and modern supplementation methods, as these are the true methods of treatment. However, it is essential to reside in a separate, peaceful space to cultivate tranquility and clarity of heart over time for complete recovery. If one does not avoid human interactions and only relies on medications while ignoring appropriate adjustments, there is a fear of extreme fluctuations that may yield no effective results. Those suffering from this condition must not be unaware![73p]
    Discussion on Inspissation and Phlegm Syndrome

    In the case of inspissation, it is definitely formed due to excessive sorrow, worry, accumulated labor, pent-up emotions, or harm from excessive indulgence in alcohol and sensual pleasures. Excessive worry and thought lead to qi stagnation; when qi is stagnant, transformation doesn’t occur. Excessive indulgence injures the yin, and if the yin is harmed, essence and blood dry up. If qi stagnates, there arises an upper syndrome of inspissation; if essence and blood dry up, there arises a lower syndrome of dryness and stagnation. Furthermore, all human visceral qi involves the reception by the stomach, the transformation by the spleen, and the kidneys serving as the foundation of water and fire, the origin of transformation. Now, if food and drink are obstructed and not functioning, or if stool is dry and cannot be passed, is this not a failure of transformation and a disorder of blood circulation? The authority governing the circulation of blood in the upper body lies with the spleen; in the lower body, it lies with the kidneys. Moreover, this syndrome is rarely seen in the young, but is more common in those who are middle-aged and weakened. This shows the distinction between the real and the illusory. Thus, in treating this condition, one must not abandon the fundamental roots in pursuit of quick paths, as success cannot be expected! Regarding the conditions of inspissation and reverse stomach (regurgitation), Dan Xi states that although they are named differently, the illnesses arise from the same source, which certainly shows their similarity.[74p]
    However, there is indeed a difference. In cases of reversed stomach, the food can still enter the body but then comes back out, hence called "reversed stomach"; in cases of blocked throat, there is an obstruction preventing food from going down, hence called "blocked throat." When food enters and then comes back out, it is due to yang deficiency that cannot transform; this can be supplemented and warmed, making treatment relatively easy. When food cannot go down, it is due to qi stagnation that cannot move; treatment may either require unblocking or assisting, thus making the process more difficult. This reflects the differences in severity.

    Furthermore, those suffering from reversed stomach often can still eat, while those with blocked throat cannot eat. The illness of blocked throat is located in the chest and upper jiao, while the illness of reversed stomach is located in the middle and lower jiao. This is a distinction in the symptoms. Therefore, the treatment for reversed stomach often needs to boost the source of fire to assist in transformation, while the treatment for blocked throat often needs to nourish the heart and spleen to alleviate the stagnation of qi. Since the symptoms are different, diagnosis and treatment should also be categorized accordingly.

    The condition of blocked throat often accompanies constipation. The "Neijing" states: "The three yang that are bound together is called the throat." Zhang Zihe states: "The three yang refers to the large intestine, small intestine, and bladder." The binding refers to heat binding.[75p]
    When there is a heat block in the small intestine, the blood vessels become dry; when there is a heat block in the large intestine, there is constipation; when there is a heat block in the bladder, the fluids are depleted. When the three yangs are blocked, there is obstruction both before and after. If the lower part is not open, it must flow upward, which causes difficulty in swallowing food; even if it goes down, it comes back up. This is due to the yang fire pushing upwards instead of descending. In my humble opinion, this interpretation is quite incorrect. The meaning of "block" in the context of the "Inner Canon" does not primarily refer to heat, as this text states: "When yin and yang are blocked, there is more yin and less yang, which is called stone water." Furthermore, it is mentioned in the discussion of pain: "Thinking causes the qi to become blocked." Is it thus correct to equate blockage with heat? Moreover, heat facilitates flow, while cold causes condensation; this is a fundamental principle of yin and yang. Indeed, frost condenses into ice only in extreme cold, and heat does not do so. This is an evident truth of the natural order. The principles of yin and yang in the human body are no different; only humans cannot fully comprehend them, leading to many misunderstandings. Furthermore, when the "Inner Canon" refers to the blockage of the three yangs, it speaks only of the small intestine and bladder, with no relation to the large intestine. The three yangs refer to the sun, with the hand representing the small intestine and the foot representing the bladder.[76p]
    The small intestine belongs to fire and the bladder to water. If fire does not transform, yang energy does not circulate and its transmission fails; if water does not transform, yin energy does not circulate, and the distinction between clarity and turbidity becomes muddled. This is the root cause of obstruction. Zihé did not discern this and ultimately attributed the obstruction of the three yang channels entirely to heat, leading future generations to universally interpret it as fire—how misguided is that!

    However, diseases associated with obstructions do not stem from a single source; in fact, both qi and blood can become obstructed, and both yang and yin can lead to their own types of obstructions. I do not mean to say that all obstructions are cold and devoid of heat; rather, yin obstructions and yang obstructions manifest differently, and this distinction must not be overlooked. Yang obstruction is indeed a heat obstruction, arising from fire's excessive disturbance of yin, causing it to congeal. This only occurs with exterior pathogens invading the interior or with true heat in the yangming channel.

    Moreover, heat obstructions must be accompanied by symptoms such as thirst, fever, and other signs, which are easily identifiable, clearly reflected in the pulse being forceful, large, slippery, and solid. If there is obstruction below but no signs of heat above, this indicates a yin obstruction—how can one claim it to be heat? The essence of yin obstruction lies in the lack of fire in the life gate, leading to inadequate transformation of essence and resulting in congealment below, with the flow of treatment stagnated. This is exclusively due to internal injuries to the blood and qi, affecting true yin, as is the case with the symptoms of food stagnation. The symptoms of food stagnation are widely recognized as stemming from internal injuries.[77p]
    At this point of internal injury, how can one assess the health of the internal organs and the vital energies, while still claiming it to be heat? Is it truly necessary for the original yang to be completely depleted before there can be another path to life? Alas! This is something I cannot comprehend, and I must clarify!

    The condition of food obstruction has often been considered by ancient scholars to be due to cold. Since Liu Hejian treated food obstruction with the Three Chengqi Decoction, Zhang Zihe has discussed the complete binding of the three yangs as being due to heat, also stating that a person's overeating does not happen suddenly: it may either injure due to food or drink, or due to heat in the stomach causing a desire to vomit. If physicians do not understand the root cause and instead prescribe aromatic herbs, cassia, pepper, and cloves, how can they assert weakness and then supplement with heat?

    For those with inherent heat, the three yangs will certainly bind, and food will easily ascend. Yet physicians still argue that the stomach is cold and cannot accept food, cauterizing with needles and moxibustion; as the three yangs become increasingly bound over time, they eventually lead to food obstruction. I find this reasoning puzzling.

    Food obstruction arises from dryness and is not fundamentally a sign of true heat. Is the Three Chengqi Decoction still applicable? Liu Hejian's view appears inaccurate. Moreover, an excess of meat and wine does not necessarily lead to food obstruction; is it the case that all who suffer from food obstruction are people with inherent heat? Zhang Zihe's perspective also seems to have its flaws.[78p]
    From then on, Danxi followed the teachings of the two Sons and critically evaluated the errors in "Jufang." He stated that the initial sickness of qi may arise from various sources: either from careless eating and drinking, or exposure to wind and rain, or internal emotional disturbances, or from overly rich food that excessively nourishes yang qi, leading to the accumulation of heat in the diaphragm. It may also come from a full constitution without sweating, or a naturally irritable temper causing liver fire to rise, resulting in stagnation of bodily fluids, which makes qi unwell. This could manifest as distension, pain, foul belching, sour regurgitation, or a sense of fullness. Without seeking the root cause, one hastily identifies it as cold and hastily administers spicy, aromatic, and drying heat remedies, experiencing temporary relief that mistakenly seems like a miraculous cure. Continuing rich flavors and failing to regulate emotions lead to a recurrence, with old issues being aggravated, momentarily relieving stagnant fluids but making them easier to accumulate again. In half a month or a month, previous ailments re-emerge. Physicians, not understanding the situation, insist it is due to cold, and revert to previous remedies for quick results, hoping for lasting effects through prolonged use to warm the spleen, strengthen the stomach, and promote the movement of stagnation, with the hope that one day there will be a clear resolution. However, they forget that the stomach is the sea of grains and can only receive when it is clear and harmonious, and the spleen is the organ of digestion that can only function properly when it is also clear and harmonious. Now, instead of gaining assistance from the offset of fragrant warming, they allow their condition to worsen, causing long-term complications. This is indeed lamentable. This is Danxi's teaching, which, when seen by later generations, is universally regarded as the utmost principle. Even in my early years, I could not help but admire it, yet now I increasingly see its errors.[79p]
    What is it? If we examine the causes of the illness described, some may indeed be true while others may not, but it is difficult to clearly differentiate them. If we rely solely on this evidence and insist on classifying it as heat, can we be free from error? Moreover, since it is stated that dry heat medicines can promptly bring relief, it surely possesses some efficacy. If dry heat can be effective, can it truly be said that only genuine heat can be effective? The spleen is antagonistic to dampness, hence dryness can be effective. Fire can nurture earth, thus heat can be effective as well. Warming dryness supports yang; this is the proper treatment of the spleen family, and to insist otherwise, leading to doubts among later generations, seems excessive. If it is said that indulging in rich flavors and emotions without moderation leads to recurrence, whose fault is this? Can it also be attributed to a mistake in medicine? Furthermore, concerning the claim that a balanced spleen and stomach can receive and transform, this is indeed a sound principle, who would dispute it? However, my understanding of balance differs from that of Danxi. But why is that? Danxi expresses concern about excessive fire; I express concern about diminishing yang. With such differences, who will believe? Please consider the principles governing heaven and humanity; how about that? What both heaven and humanity rely upon is solely this yang qi. Thus, the text states: Clear and tranquil weather is illuminating. It also says: Yang qi is like heaven and sun; if it loses its position, it withers and does not manifest, thus heavenly operations should shine with sunlight.[80p]
    From this, it can be said that the universe thrives in harmony, with the sun playing a significant role. When the yang energy prevails, it brings warmth and light, allowing all things to flourish; is this not harmony? When the yin energy prevails, it causes wind and haze, resulting in stagnation; is this not a departure from harmony? Moreover, the flourishing of all things in spring and summer arises from the vigor of yang; while the decline of all things in autumn and winter is a sign of waning yang. What sustains human life is nothing but this.

    Thus, when food enters in the morning, it is digested by noon; when it enters the stomach by noon, it is digested by evening. The heat within, similar to that of a boiling cauldron, must align with harmony. This is the normal state of a person and is precisely what we call the stomach's energy. If one eats in the morning and is not hungry by noon, eats at noon and is not hungry by evening, and if digestion is slow, this is an indication of diminished yang energy.

    Moreover, if one finds oneself completely unable to function or digest, doctors would still assert there is fire; must this source of transformation be entirely extinguished before one can be well again? It is truly lamentable! The principles of the world are not numerous, yet why do we find so much divergence of opinion? I often wish to speak, but I am always aware of my own biases. Yet at the most perplexing points, I cannot but present the ugly truths. How can I maintain my own correctness without causing further conflict?

    I have often heard the sage Mr. Kangjie say: to be a dragon-slayer in the world, one must not shy away from reading texts that are not sacred. This is deeply resonant. Is this not true? Is this not true?[81p]
    In the treatment of phlegm obstruction, the spleen and kidneys should be the main focus. The spleen governs transformation and transportation, and its extensive networks spread throughout the chest region; the kidneys govern fluids, with their energy regulating the lower yin. Therefore, the obstruction in the upper jiao is attributed to the spleen, while the blockage in the lower jiao is attributed to the kidneys. To treat the spleen, one should proceed from warming nourishment, while to treat the kidneys, one should focus on moistening. Beyond these two methods, there are no shortcuts. However, the principle of balance indicates that the heavens and earth are interconnected, and thus, the three yang must emerge from the ground before all things thrive, illustrating that the mother of spleen earth rises from below.

    Chancellor Chu said: "To treat external diseases, address internal issues; to treat upper ailments, support the lower." One must discern the deficiency or excess of the organs and understand the dynamic between parent and child organs in disease. This wisdom is valuable and should not be overlooked.

    For treating phlegm obstruction, when both qi and blood are deficient, one should use the Five Blessings Decoction or the Ten Complete Great Nourishing Soup. If there is spleen deficiency in the upper body, one should use the Four Gentlemen Decoction; if there is spleen deficiency with cold, the Five Gentlemen Decoction is indicated. If there is deficiency in the spleen and lung with dry blood, one should use ginger juice decoction; for lower yin deficiency, one should use the Left Return Decoction or the Great Nourishing Decoction. If there is yin deficiency with yang deficiency, one should use the Right Return Decoction with angelica, or formulas like the Right Return Pill or the Eight Flavor Rehmannia Pill. These are all foundational treatment methods.[82p]
    When experiencing initial symptoms of obstruction, where there is slight weakness, it is advisable to prepare a warm stomach drink with added angelica and magnolia bark. If there is phlegm and qi not cleared, with stagnation in the upper diaphgram, a combination of Er Chen Tang (Two Evolved Decoction) with added magnolia bark is recommended, or Liu'an decoction may also be suitable. If there are signs of qi not flowing smoothly, or if there is slight pain in the chest and abdomen, adding or subtracting from Er Chen Tang to alleviate those symptoms is appropriate.

    For those who initially feel that food is not digesting well, and who are not severely aged, a prompt use of Da Jian Pi Wan (Great Spleen Strengthening Pill), or pills made with musk, ginseng, ginger, and zhigao (bitter orange), to regulate spleen qi is the best strategy; Shaoyao Zhi Zhu Wan (Pill with Peony and Bitter Orange) may also work.

    If there is both obstruction and constipation due to lack of fire and stagnation, merely observe for dryness of blood and deficiency of yin. In this case, Wufu Drink or Daying Decoction, with a few coins of alcohol to wash meat cistanche, should be decocted and consumed. Alternatively, using pig gall to gradually moisten from below while treating the upper part with spleen-regulating remedies is the best approach.

    One may also consume more cow or sheep milk products to nourish the essence and gradually moisturize, avoiding any impatience! If both qi and blood are not greatly depleted but there is significant stagnation in the lower abdomen, it may become necessary to temporarily alleviate it. For mild cases, Yuzhu San, Ren Shen Li Ge Wan, or Soufeng Shunqi Wan may be suitable; for severe cases, Da Huang Gan Cao Tang should be used appropriately.[83p]
    Using warming and tonifying methods to treat obstruction of the throat, one might suspect stagnation and find it too slow, not realizing the collapse of central qi. This is most concerning: if the root cause isn't swiftly remedied, how can the spleen's qi be restored? If warming and tonifying methods cause greater obstruction, one must reluctantly adjust the treatment. Yet, it is crucial to carefully select from the foundational qi to ensure safety.

    Even if after using tonics no immediate effect is seen, as long as there is no blockage, it indicates a fitting combination of medicine and ailment. This condition is particularly difficult to treat. If the patient can tolerate tonics, they must take them in substantial amounts to gradually see results and achieve full recovery. One shouldn't be impatient and suspicious, causing self-doubt and fluctuation in treatment effectiveness.

    If urgent remedies are sought for immediate relief, employing drastic measures such as Da Huang (rhubarb), Mang Xiao (bitter salt), San Leng (sparganii rhizome), E Zhu (curcuma), Yu Jin (curcuma), Tao Ren (peach kernel), or rolling phlegm pills will not yield results; they will only damage gastric qi, leading to inevitable deterioration. This is hastening towards demise and must be carefully considered.

    In treating throat obstruction, traditional practices rely on ginseng and astragalus to tonify the original qi, while using rice and millet to detoxify and strengthen the stomach, bamboo juice to clear phlegm and disperse masses, dried ginger to warm the center, fresh ginger to eliminate impurities, cow and sheep milk to nourish blood and moisten fluids, as well as honey and angelica to relieve dryness. These are the primary treatments, with adjustments based on the symptoms as necessary, all of which are sound methods.[84p]
    For those who are obese, instances of choking are rare; when they do occur, it is advisable to use Erchen Decoction with ginseng and white atractylodes. For individuals who are blood-deficient and weak, use Siwu Decoction combined with Erchen Decoction, adding peach kernels, safflower, leek juice, child's urine, and cow or sheep milk. If choking is caused by emotional distress, combine Erchen Decoction with xiangfu, angelica, mu xiang, betel nut, and sand ginger. For those who drink alcohol and suffer from choking, add huanglian, sand ginger, and sugar to Erchen Decoction. If there is heat in the chest, add huanglian, huangqin, jie geng, and sand ginger. If the spleen is insufficiently nourished, add shenqu, sand ginger, and malt to assist digestion. For severe dry constipation accompanying choking, use da huang, or apply Erchen Decoction with wine to steam da huang and peach kernels for lubrication, as this is an emergency measure. Alternatively, use Siwu Decoction with peach kernels, child's urine, leek juice, and drink plenty of cow or sheep milk as the best strategy.

    According to ancient methods for treating choking, these principles have been largely covered here. Although there are appropriate and inappropriate treatments among them, all are documented for selection. Generally, older individuals suffering from this condition are often difficult to treat due to deficient blood and qi. If the stool resembles sheep dung, it is untreatable, as the large intestine has no blood. If the phlegm is like crab foam, it is also untreatable, reflecting depletion of spleen qi. Abdominal pain that feels like being cut by knives indicates an extreme deficiency of nutrients, and it is untreatable as blood is exhausted within.[85p]
    The Argument of Reflux

    The phenomenon of reflux is fundamentally due to a deficiency of fire. When food enters the stomach, it warms the spleen and strengthens it, leading to complete digestion; how could it then be rejected? Various schools of thought suggest it is due to phlegm or excess heat. They do not realize that the retention of phlegm and fluid stems from a weakness in the stomach, causing undigested food to be expelled — how could it be considered properly digested? Wang Taibu said, "If there is obstruction in the stomach causing nausea and food cannot be taken in, this indicates the presence of fire; but if food comes back up after being ingested, this indicates a lack of fire." This statement is indeed comprehensive. However, even without fire, it is essential to discern between the upper, middle, and lower jiaos, as different conditions apply.

    If cold is present in the upper jiao, it often results in nausea or a general feeling of wanting to vomit; this indicates a deficiency of yang in the stomach. If cold is in the middle jiao, then the food does not digest, and may be expelled shortly after, indicating a deficiency of yang within the stomach. If cold is in the lower jiao, it can lead to morning vomiting of food eaten the previous evening, or evening vomiting of food eaten in the morning; this occurs because food enters the pylorus but cannot be transformed by the fire, resulting in expulsion due to prolonged retention, indicative of a deficiency of yang at the life gate (ming men). Thus, for any treatment of this condition, if one does not understand where the root of the problem lies, they may blindly guess and aim for results without understanding the underlying pathology, making it exceedingly difficult.[86p]
    To treat the condition of reverse stomach (nausea), it is essential to distinguish between its recent and long-standing causes. It may stem from excessive drinking leading to dampness from alcohol, or from overeating cold foods that deplete the true yang. It could also be due to emotional distress that exhausts the internal energy. In summary, this is primarily a case of internal injury, which weakens the stomach energy. Therefore, any treatment for this should focus on supporting the righteous energy, strengthening the spleen, and nourishing the stomach.

    For new cases where the stomach energy has not yet deteriorated significantly, if the food has not been digested, one should also remove the stagnation. If there is still rebellious qi, it is essential to also resolve the stagnation. In cases that have persisted for a longer time, or for those with inherent weak energy, one should specifically apply warming and reinforcing treatments. It is crucial to avoid mixing treatments improperly, recklessly using harsh and drastic remedies such as those for digesting food and resolving phlegm, which could severely injure the stomach energy and lead to further complications.

    For conditions characterized by deficiency in the upper jiao with mild cold and nausea, ginger soup is the best choice, and tangerine peel soup may also be suitable.[87p]
    If qi deficiency is invaded by cold, leading to nausea and vomiting of food, it is advisable to use Huangya Wan or Jiangpi Ganjiang Tang. If cold phlegm is predominant, Xiao Banxia Tang or Da Banxia Tang should be employed.

    If the deficiency is in the middle jiao and food is returned after a long time, take Wujunzhi Jian, Lizhong Tang, Wenge Yinian or Shengzhu Jian. If the stomach is severely deficient, use Siwei Huoyang Yin or Huangya Wan. If combined with cold phlegm, opt for Liu Junzi Tang or Lizhong Huatan Wan. If there is phlegm due to fluid retention, Jinshui Liu Jun Jian should be used.

    If the stomach is not severely cold but slightly deficient with stagnation, Wuwai Yigong San is recommended.

    If the deficiency is in the lower jiao, and there is vomiting of breakfast by evening, or food taken in a long time is regurgitated, the problem lies in yin. Unless one nourishes the life gate to support the spleen, there will be no fire for transformation and no earth for generation, akin to a pot without fuel, incapable of cooking grains and ultimately yielding no benefit.[88p]
    One should use the Six Flavor Rehmannia Decoction, or combine ginseng and aconite for a yin-regulating brew, or utilize the Right Returning Decoction and similar remedies to address the issue. This is a frequently employed effective method that must not be overlooked!

    When the symptoms of counterflow have just begun, and the qi is strong, one may first proceed with purification. Decocting formulas like Erchen Decoction or Chenpi Banxia Decoction can clear phlegm and regulate qi, while Pingwei Powder, Bujing Zhenqi Powder, and Wuling Powder can eliminate dampness and alleviate stagnation. Additionally, Banxia Gancao Decoction, Zhongjing Wuzhuyu Decoction, and Chenpi Decoction can dispel cold. However, these remedies should only be employed when truly obstructive pathogens are present. If the condition has persisted for some time and the gastric qi is weak, these remedies are not advisable.

    It is common for counterflow symptoms to coincide with constipation, as the upward flow is hindered by obstruction below.[89p]
    If the lower passages are obstructed, how can it not be a reflection of the upper energy not transforming? It is because the spleen and stomach have weakened qi; thus, the cycle of circulation does not function, and there is insufficient blood production, leading to a stagnation below which causes blockage. This is a true manifestation of desiccation of the yin. It is essential to gradually nourish the blood and qi and moisten the internal organs, as this is the fundamental treatment. If one merely seeks immediate results without addressing the root cause, there may be temporary relief, but the true yin will become further depleted. Therefore, in treating this condition, if one observes deficiency of yin along with cold, the main approach should be to tonify the yang, significantly incorporating ingredients like angelica, cistanche, leek juice, and ginger juice. If there is deficiency of yin accompanied by heat, the main approach should be to nourish the yin, adding substances like milk, children's urine, ghee, honey, pig fat, and various types of blood. However, these treatments are particularly difficult to yield results, and one must not desire quick fixes. Without a period of careful adjustment, usually spanning several weeks or months, recovery cannot be achieved! Conditions presenting with feces resembling sheep droppings, or those who are older and suffer from this ailment, are especially challenging to treat. For instances of reversed stomach due to alcohol dampening the spleen, the main prescription should be Ge Hua Jie Cheng Decoction. If there is excess dampness leading to heat, with symptoms of stomach fire surging upwards, Huang Qin Decoction or Ban Xia Xie Xin Decoction should be administered.[90p]
    Secret Formula of the Stone Chamber
    [Qing Dynasty - Chen Shiduo]

    Treatment Method for Reflux and Nausea

    At the onset of reflux symptoms, it is not necessarily a stomach issue. At this point, taking Xiao Yao San with ten grams of Huai Lian can stop it. However, many doctors do not understand the treatment method and instead use ingredients like Xiang Sha, Hou Po, Zhi Ke, and Sha Ren, which do not work; they then switch to using Da Huang and Ba Dou, which also fail. Next, they try cooling herbs like Huang Lian, Huang Bo, Huang Qin, Zhi Zi, and Zhi Mu, but this too is ineffective. They try warming herbs such as Gui Zhi, Bai Guo, Rou Gui, Fu Zi, Gan Jiang, and Wu Zhu Yu, yet there is still no response. Finally, they use dispersing herbs like Chai Hu, Jing Jie, Jie Geng, Fang Feng, and Su Zi, resulting in the condition of reflux.

    Now, I pass on a formula: one ounce of Shu Di, four coins of Shan Zhu Yu, three coins of Mai Dong, one coin each of Bei Wu Wei, Yuan Shen, Dang Gui, and Bai Jie Zi, and two coins of Niu Xi, boiled in water for consumption. The brilliance of this formula lies in not treating the reflux directly, which is precisely how to treat it. The reason for reflux is the depletion of Yin water in the kidneys. When kidney water is insufficient, the large and small intestines cannot be sufficiently moistened, leading to dryness. With dry intestines, food entering the stomach cannot descend and must instead be vomited back up. Therefore, the method of treatment should not focus on the upper part but rather on the lower.[91p]
    The formula employs prepared rehmannia and shan zhu yu, which are purely for replenishing the water in the kidneys. When kidney water is sufficient, the large intestine is also nourished with water, allowing it to expand and properly accommodate substances. When the pathway for water is wide, boats of all sizes can navigate freely; hence, there is no fear that a small boat cannot transport grain. This treatment is appropriate for a deficiency of water in the kidneys that fails to moisten the large intestine. However, if the kidneys are cold and deficient, this method is not suitable. Although the name "reversal of stomach" may be the same, the actual condition differs. Reversal due to a lack of water in the kidneys causes immediate vomiting upon swallowing food, while reversal from a lack of fire in the kidneys results in delayed vomiting, where food is retained until the next day before being expelled. For example, food eaten today may only be vomited out the next day.

    The formula includes one tael of prepared rehmannia, four qian of shan zhu yu, three qian of mai dong, two qian each of shan yao and fu ling, one qian each of northern wuweizi, dan pi, ze xie, niu xi, fu zi, and rou gui, and is decocted in water for consumption. This is the eight-ingredient pill soup. The key lies in using fu zi and rou gui to nourish kidney water while also addressing the fire within the water. Nourishing the fire helps to rejuvenate the life-gate fire. The qi of the spleen and stomach must rely on the life-gate fire to be generated; like a pot without fire, how can it cook? Without it, the water is cold and metal is chilled, leading to frost. One must first restore the initial warmth of yang to resolve this.[92p]
    The human body’s spleen and stomach function similarly; however, in the case of illnesses caused by cold and chill, one usually experiences abdominal pain and distress. Yet, if there are no such symptoms now, what could be the reason for the upward sensation accompanied by vomiting? It is because when the spleen and stomach have a passage, the cold pathogenic energy does not remain within. Now, with the upward forces releasing through the mouth and throat, the gastric entrance has become a broad avenue, allowing for free passage without obstruction. Thus, the energy easily flows, and there is no need for it to accumulate within the central region, lurking in the heart and abdomen, causing cold to generate heat that brings suffering to the spleen and stomach. This, in fact, is what allows for the absence of pain in the heart and abdomen. This is not merely about treating the symptoms of vomiting but rather addressing the root cause of it; this marks the beginning of transformative methods for treating diseases.

    When food enters and quickly exits, it is due to the deficiency of kidney water, which fails to moisten the throat, leading to dryness and an immediate expulsion. When food lingers and then is expelled, it is due to the deficiency of kidney fire, which cannot warm the spleen, resulting in cold in the spleen and subsequent expulsion. To treat the symptoms of vomiting, it is essential to address the kidneys and to differentiate whether there is fire or no fire; doing so can transform potentially fatal symptoms into life-sustaining outcomes. The ideal remedy for the symptoms of vomiting is to use Zhang Jing’s Rehmannia Decoction. If there is no fire, one should add Aconite and Cinnamon, which will yield beneficial results just as before. However, some people may use Zhang Jing’s prescriptions without achieving results. Why is this? It is due to improperly applying the method, rather than any deficiency in the formulas themselves. Now, regarding the two prescriptions: one for treating vomiting without fire includes one ounce of cooked Rehmannia, one ounce of Sichuan Cornelian cherry, and three qian each of Aconite, Poria, Water Plantain, Moutan Peony, and Cinnamon, along with one qian of Chinese Yam, to be decocted and consumed.[93p]
    For treating fire and regurgitation, use: 2 taels of cooked rehmannia, 1 tael of yam, 3 qian each of alisma and processed moutan root, 5 qian each of poria, ophiopogon, and cornelian cherry, and 2 qian of northen schisandra, decoct in water and take. Adjustments can be made to this formula naturally, effectively treating regurgitation.

    The symptoms of regurgitation, though not immediately fatal, if treated improperly, can lead to death. Regurgitation is often due to kidney deficiency without fire. Therefore, if food is eaten today, it may be vomited by tomorrow, as stated in the "Inner Canon" that food consumes the person then exits. Food entering the stomach and then being vomited seems like a stomach problem. However, the kidneys serve as the gatekeeper for the stomach; if the kidneys are ill, the stomach will also become ill. For food to enter the stomach, it must be aided by kidney fluids, and the throat must have pathways for moisture to enable proper digestion.

    Without fire in the kidneys, there is no fuel for the pot, so how can it steam the water and grain? This leads to cold in the kidneys and spleen; a cold spleen cannot transform food, causing it to rise to the stomach, which cannot accept it, resulting in vomiting. The formula for settling the stomach includes: 3 taels of cooked rehmannia, 2 taels of cornelian cherry, 3 qian each of cinnamon and poria, decoct in water and take. One dose can stop vomiting, and ten can completely cure the illness. This treatment is for those who vomit from morning to night, and for those who vomit right after meals, do not use cinnamon. Instead, add 1 tael of ophiopogon and 1 qian of northen schisandra; this adjustment has proven effective. Both formulas are great for nourishing the water and fire in the kidneys without directly treating the stomach, proving to be more effective than treating the stomach itself.[94p]
    Prescription

    Major Banxia Decoction (Zhongjing, same below) treats gastric reversal and vomiting. Use two sheng of Banxia, three liang of Ren Shen, and one sheng of honey. Take these three ingredients, add twelve sheng of water, mix with honey, and stir for two hundred forty times. Boil the medicine to yield two and a half sheng, warm and take one sheng, dividing into two doses.

    Minor Banxia Decoction treats vomiting where food cannot be retained. Use one sheng of Banxia and half a jin of fresh ginger. Take these two ingredients, add seven sheng of water, boil to yield one and a half sheng, and take warm in two doses.[95p]
    Dahuang Gancao Decoction: Treats those who vomit immediately after eating.
    Ingredients: 4 liang of Dahuang, 1 liang of Gancao.
    Preparation: Combine both ingredients with 3 sheng of water, boil to obtain 1 sheng, and take in two warm doses.

    Fuling Zexie Decoction: Treats thirst and desire to drink water after stomach issues.
    Ingredients: 0.5 jin of Fuling, 4 liang each of Zexie and fresh ginger, 3 liang of Baizhu, 2 liang each of Gui Zhi and Gancao.
    Preparation: Boil the six ingredients in 10 dou of water to obtain 3 sheng, then add Zexie and boil again to get 2.5 sheng. Take 8 he warm three times a day.

    Wuyeyuan: Treats prolonged cold in the chest leading to nausea, reverse flow of qi affecting appetite, and stubborn qi stagnation.
    Ingredients: 0.5 liang each of dried ginger, Sichuan pepper, Shanzhuyu, and heart of cinnamon, 5 fen each of Ginseng, Fuxi, Baizhu, Poria, and 4 fen of Ziziphus; 6 fen of tangerine peel.
    Preparation: Grind all ten ingredients into a powder, mix with honey to form pills the size of wutong seeds; take three pills with wine three times a day. If ineffective, gradually increase to ten pills.

    Another Formula: Treats five types of qi that cause choking.
    Ingredients: 2 liang each of Ginseng, Banxia, and heart of cinnamon, 2 liang of Fangfeng, 2 liang of a small herb, 2 liang of Fuxi, 2 liang of Xixin, 2 liang of Gancao; 6 fen of Zihuan and dried ginger, 6 fen of Shanzhuyu, 6 fen of Baishao, 6 fen of Wutou; 1 liang of Zhi Shi.
    Preparation: Grind all fourteen ingredients into a powder, mix with honey to form pills the size of wutong seeds; take five pills with wine three times a day. If ineffective, increase to fifteen pills. Wutou and Banxia counteract each other, so just omit one ingredient for the combination.[96p]
    Bamboo Skin Decoction treats inability to speak due to choking.

    Ingredients: Bamboo skin (also known as bamboo leaves), dried ginger, each 2 liang; licorice, fresh ginger,通草 (tongcao), ginseng, poria, ephedra, five-flavor fruit, cinnamon heart, each 1 liang.

    Preparation: Grind the ten ingredients together, using 10 dou of water to cook the bamboo skin until it reduces by 2 sheng. Remove the bamboo skin, add the remaining ingredients, cook to yield 3 sheng, and divide into three doses.

    Dried Ginger Decoction treats choking from eating and drinking, also called Banxia Decoction.

    Ingredients: Dried ginger, gypsum, each 4 liang; banxia, wheat, each 1 sheng; ginseng, cinnamon heart, loofah root, each 2 liang; licorice 1 liang; Wu zhu yu 2 sheng; 30 red beans.

    Preparation: Grind the ten ingredients together, using 5 sheng of wine and 20 dou of water, cooking with 20 dates. After straining, combine the decoction and reduce to 3 sheng, and divide into three doses.

    Qi Unblocking Decoction treats fullness in the chest with choking.

    Ingredients: Banxia 8 liang; fresh ginger 6 liang; cinnamon heart 3 liang; 30 jujubes.

    Preparation: Grind the four ingredients together, using 8 sheng of water to cook until yielding 3 sheng, and divide into five doses, taken three times during the day and twice at night.

    Antelope Horn Decoction treats unyielding choking, preventing food intake.

    Ingredients: Antelope horn, 通草 (tongcao), tangerine peel, each 2 liang; magnolia bark, Wu zhu yu, dried ginger, each 3 liang; five black codonopsis roots.

    Preparation: Grind the seven ingredients together, using 9 sheng of water to cook until yielding 3 sheng, and divide into three doses, taken three times a day.

    Reversal Food Method treats empty stomach causing regurgitation, resulting in immediate vomiting.[97p]
    Ren Shen (ginseng) 1 tael, Ze Xie (alisma) 2 taels, Gan Cao ( licorice) 2 taels, Gui Xin (cinnamon) 2 taels, Ju Pi (orange peel) 3 taels, Gan Jiang (dried ginger) 3 taels, Fu Ling (poria) 4 taels, Da Huang (rhubarb) 6 taels, Zhu Ru (bamboo shavings) 5 taels.

    Take these nine ingredients, chop them, and add 8 sheng (a measure of liquid), boil down to 3 sheng. Take 7 he (a measure of liquid) per dose, three times a day and once at night. If diarrhea occurs, stop using Da Huang.

    Another prescription treats rebellious stomach qi and thirst:
    Fu Ling, Ze Xie, and Ban Xia (pinellia), each 4 taels; Gui Xin and Gan Cao, each 2 taels. Chop these five ingredients, add 5 sheng water, boil down to 2 sheng, and divide into three doses. One version adds 4 taels of Sheng Jiang (fresh ginger).

    Another prescription treats stomach rebelliousness, where food is not digested, causing continuous vomiting:
    Ren Shen, Ze Xie, and Gui Xin, each 2 taels; Fu Ling 4 taels; Ju Pi, Gan Cao, and Huang Qi (astragalus), each 3 taels; Da Huang 1.5 taels; Sheng Jiang 8 taels; Ban Xia 1 sheng; Mai Men Dong (ophiopogon) 3 sheng.

    Chop these eleven ingredients, add 12 sheng water, boil down to 3 sheng and 2 he, take 8 he per dose, three times a day and once at night. For weak individuals, take it in six doses. If diarrhea occurs, stop using Da Huang.

    Another prescription treats stomach counterflow, with morning food and evening vomiting, sharp abdominal pain after eating, due to prolonged exposure to cold:
    Ju Pi 3 taels; Gan Cao, Hou Po (magnolia bark), Fu Ling, Gui Xin, Xi Xin (asarum), Xing Ren (apricot kernels), and Zhu Pi (bamboo skin), each 2 taels; Bing Lang (betel nut) 10 pieces, Qian Hu (peucedanum) 8 taels; Sheng Jiang 5 taels; Ren Shen 1 tael.

    Chop these twelve ingredients, add 13 sheng water, boil down to 3 sheng, and divide into three doses. One version includes 2 taels of Gan Pi (sweet orange peel).[98p]
